        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.4c05252"><meta property="og:title" content="Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/acsami.4c05252.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Self-healing functional materials can repair cracks and damage inside the battery, ensuring the stability of the battery material structure. This feature minimizes performance degradation during the charging and discharging processes, improving the efficiency and stability of the battery. Here, we have developed a novel healing conductive two-dimensional sulfur iodide (SI4) composite cathode. This process integrates both sulfur and iodine compounds into carbon nanocages, forming a SI4@C core–shell structure. This cathode design improves electrical conductivity and repairability, facilitates rapid activation, and ensures structural integrity, resulting in a typical Na–SI4 battery with high capacity and an exceptionally long cycle life. At 10.0 A g–1, the capacity of the Na–SI4 battery can still reach 217.4 mAh g–1 after more than 500 cycles, and the capacity decay rate per cycle is only 0.06%. In addition, the cathode exhibits a cascade redox reaction involving S and I, contributing to its high capacity. The in situ growth of a carbon shell further enhances the conductivity and structural robustness of the entire cathode. The flexibility and bendability of SI4@C-carbon cloth make it applicable for flexible electronic devices, providing more possibilities for battery design. The strategy of engineering a two-dimensional self-healing structure to construct a superior cathode is expected to be widely applied to other electrode materials. This study provides a new pathway for designing novel binary-conversion-type sodium-ion batteries with excellent long-term cycling performance."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/acsami.4c05252.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318846f1b2154b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318846f1b2154b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'c628bd7d-37bd-4768-b9fe-9b593517d4f9'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318846f1b2154b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318846f1b2154b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Healable and Conductive Two-Dimensional Sulfur Iodide for High-Rate Sodium Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.4c05252", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">5</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/medium/am4c05252_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c05252&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Self-healing functional materials can repair cracks and damage inside the battery, ensuring the stability of the battery material structure. This feature minimizes performance degradation during the charging and discharging processes, improving the efficiency and stability of the battery. Here, we have developed a novel healing conductive two-dimensional sulfur iodide (SI<sub>4</sub>) composite cathode. This process integrates both sulfur and iodine compounds into carbon nanocages, forming a SI<sub>4</sub>@C core–shell structure. This cathode design improves electrical conductivity and repairability, facilitates rapid activation, and ensures structural integrity, resulting in a typical Na–SI<sub>4</sub> battery with high capacity and an exceptionally long cycle life. At 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity of the Na–SI<sub>4</sub> battery can still reach 217.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after more than 500 cycles, and the capacity decay rate per cycle is only 0.06%. In addition, the cathode exhibits a cascade redox reaction involving S and I, contributing to its high capacity. The <i>in situ</i> growth of a carbon shell further enhances the conductivity and structural robustness of the entire cathode. The flexibility and bendability of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth make it applicable for flexible electronic devices, providing more possibilities for battery design. The strategy of engineering a two-dimensional self-healing structure to construct a superior cathode is expected to be widely applied to other electrode materials. This study provides a new pathway for designing novel binary-conversion-type sodium-ion batteries with excellent long-term cycling performance.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D32291%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMengmeng%2BQian%252C%2BFeng%2BWu%252C%2BJunfan%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D25%26contentID%3Dacsami.4c05252%26title%3DHealable%2Band%2BConductive%2BTwo-Dimensional%2BSulfur%2BIodide%2Bfor%2BHigh-Rate%2BSodium%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D32297%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290687&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical conductivity"> Electrical conductivity </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Self healing materials"> Self healing materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials"> Two dimensional materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Two-dimensional&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_two-dimensional">two-dimensional</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=SI+Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_SI%20cathode">SI cathode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Multielectron&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_multielectron">multielectron</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cascaded+Redox&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cascaded%20redox">cascaded redox</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20battery">sodium battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Human’s overexploitation and irrational utilization of nonrenewable resources have indeed led to severe energy shortages and environmental crises. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> The shift to the development and utilization of renewable resources is an important step in addressing many environmental and resource issues. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> Advanced energy storage devices such as lithium/sodium/potassium (Li/Na/K) batteries, magnesium/calcium/zinc-ion batteries, and supercapacitors have witnessed rapid advancements in recent years. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Despite these strides, these energy storage solutions have encountered several challenges. One prominent challenge is the continuous expansion and contraction of electrode materials within these devices, such as sulfur, silicon, and Li/Na/K metal. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9 to 12">(9−12)</a> This phenomenon occurs during the cycling process as a result of the insertion and extraction of numerous metal ions. The consequential formation of cracks and pulverization diminishes the cycling performance of these energy storage devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Moreover, flexible and wearable electronic products are susceptible to various forms of damage, including impact, bending, stretching, folding, and distortion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 14 to 16">(14−16)</a> These actions can inflict internal harm on energy storage devices, with resulting damages often elusive and challenging to rectify. Such damage can lead to structural compromise, leakage, short circuits, and in extreme cases, explosions of energy storage devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> Consequently, a critical challenge in this domain is the fabrication of energy storage devices that are resilient to deformation and wear. In response to this challenge, scientists have turned to self-healing materials as a source of inspiration. By leveraging self-healing materials, researchers have devised energy storage devices equipped with self-repair capabilities, markedly enhancing their longevity and safety. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 19 to 21">(19−21)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Despite their abundant resources, low cost, environmental friendliness, high theoretical capacity (S: 1675 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), high output voltage (I<sub>2</sub>: 2.9 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li), and good electrochemical reversibility, the cycling stability of Na–S and Na–I<sub>2</sub> batteries is poor due to the severe dissolution of polysulfides and polyiodides in the electrolyte during the charge–discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 22 to 26">(22−26)</a> More critically, during charge and discharge, S and iodine materials undergo significant volume expansion and contraction when they chemically react with sodium at the cathode. This volume expansion issue can lead to structural damage of electrode materials, thereby reducing the battery’s cycling stability and lifespan. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> In recent years, self-healing materials have been shown to be effective in increasing the cycle life of the electrodes. Various liquid materials that use fluidity and surface tension to achieve self-healing have been developed. For example, liquid metal materials are ideal for self-healing due to their liquid state, self-healing ability, and surface tension. Gallium is very interesting as an anode material with a low melting point (29.76 °C), which can accommodate two Li atoms when fully lithiated, providing a theoretical capacity of 769 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Sulfur-iodide (S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub>) materials are considered promising due to their intrinsic conductivity and low melting point, which can facilitate the formation and reformation of liquid/solid interfaces. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 30 to 37">(30−37)</a> These characteristics make sulfur-iodide (S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub>) materials potentially useful in electrochemical energy storage systems such as solid-state Li–S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> batteries. During the charge and discharge process, S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> undergoes solid–liquid phase transitions, making the interface between liquid S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> and solid electrolyte crucial. The intrinsic conductivity of S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> materials can enhance electron transfer, while their low melting point helps in forming better liquid/solid interfaces, thereby improving battery performance and cycling stability. Therefore, the perfect contact of liquid–solid interfaces is crucial in many applications, especially in electronic devices and energy storage systems.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Typically, the S<sub>9.3</sub>I molecular crystal composed of S and I<sub>2</sub> represents a composite material, exhibiting excellent semiconductor-grade conductivity, with its conductivity surpassing S by several orders of magnitude. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The introduction of I<sub>2</sub> into the electrochemical process creates a new state within the bandgap, promoting the formation of active polysulfides. In addition, due to the relatively low melting point of the material, which is about 65 °C, periodic remelting cycles can be carried out to repair damaged interfaces, providing a significant advantage for cathode materials. Therefore, addressing these challenges by continuously exploring the characteristics of novel self-healing electrode materials and attempting to develop applications for new electrode materials can advance the science and technology of secondary batteries. Herein, we have designed S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> composite materials, in which the combination of S and I<sub>2</sub> enhances the overall material conductivity and lowers the melting point. Periodic remelting of the S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> material can repair interfaces damaged by cycling.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">According to literature reports, typically, I<sub>2</sub> forms a purple solution in CS<sub>2</sub> solvent with a low dielectric constant, and CIS, CS<sub>2</sub>I<sup>+</sup>, and CSI<sup>–</sup> are formed in mixtures of CS<sub>2</sub> and I<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> However, S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> is a thermodynamically metastable material rather than a dynamically stabilized one. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 34, 38 and 39">(34,38,39)</a> Here, inspired by the excellent healability and conductivity of S<sub>9.3</sub>I, we propose a new thermal synchronization construction strategy to construct S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> mixture cathodes in a one-step reaction using the excellent solubility of I<sub>2</sub> in a CS<sub>2</sub> solution. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a, when heated in a CS<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, molten I<sub>2</sub> is converted to S–I, while gaseous CS<sub>2</sub> is reduced to C. The carbon product densely coated the surface of the S–I compound, forming a compact S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub>@C core–shell nanostructure, which can reduce the direct contact between the active materials and the electrolyte and improve the stability of S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub>. The I:S atomic ratio was determined by inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS), which is very close to 4:1 reaction stoichiometry (denoted as SI<sub>4</sub>). This novel Na–SI<sub>4</sub> cascaded battery structure sequentially combines two equivalent redox reactions in a single battery chamber, aiming to achieve high theoretical capacity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) and high voltage, ultimately realizing a high energy output of Na-based batteries. This design is intended to enhance battery performance and contribute to the development of Na-based battery technology.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/medium/am4c05252_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Fabrication process of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth electrodes and the (b–d) SEM images of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth at different magnifications.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c05252&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The melting point of SI<sub>4</sub> was studied by differential scanning calorimetry (DSC). The low melting point of SI<sub>4</sub> (approximately 65 °C) compared to the melting points of S (112.0 °C) and I<sub>2</sub> (113.7 °C) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) demonstrates that the incorporation of S disrupts the van der Waals interactions in I<sub>2</sub>, helping to lower the melting point and allowing the cathode to periodically heal through melting. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> In order to achieve rapid electron and ion transport while accommodating high-loading active materials, we prepared a novel binder-less, independently flexible SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth electrode by <i>in situ</i> nucleation of two-dimensional (2D) SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheets on a three-dimensional (3D) structured carbon cloth (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b–d). The carbon cloth consists of 3D interconnected thick carbon fibers with a smooth surface that does not contain any impurities, and its unique 3D conductive network provides a considerable specific surface area that facilitates electrolyte wetting (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). As a comparison, we prepared a physically mixed S/I<sub>2</sub> complex electrode coated on aluminum foil (denoted as S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil). The wettability of pure-carbon cloth, S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil, and SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth in the electrolyte solution was evaluated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil exhibits an electrolyte contact angle of about 112.6°, indicating its weak affinity for liquid electrolytes. The pure carbon cloth shows an electrolyte contact angle of about 93°, indicating its relatively strong affinity for liquid electrolytes. After modification, the affinity between SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth and electrolyte is enhanced, and the contact angle is about 32.1°, which is conducive to the diffusion of electrolyte. Moreover, the 3D carbon cloth creates room for volume expansion and the accumulation of Na<sub>2</sub>S/NaI. The utilization of 2D SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheets holds significant promise due to their outstanding electrical conductivity and electron mobility. This characteristic can substantially enhance the efficiency of electron transfer within batteries, leading to a reduction in internal resistance. Consequently, this improvement boosts both the charge–discharge efficiency and overall performance of the cell. Additionally, the 2D nature of SI<sub>4</sub>@C materials results in exceptionally high specific surface areas, creating a plethora of active sites for electrochemical reactions. This increased surface area facilitates greater contact between the electrode and electrolyte, thereby enhancing the electrochemical performance of the battery.</div><div class="NLM_p">We characterized the crystal structure and chemical composition of SI<sub>4</sub>@C using scanning electron microscopy (SEM), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and atomic force microscope (AFM). The SEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a clearly demonstrates that the prepared product comprises 2D nanosheets with lateral dimensions ranging from 0.5 to 1.0 μm. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b exhibits a low-magnification image of a polygonal-shaped SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheet, showcasing the high crystallinity of the 2D SI<sub>4</sub>@C material. The corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) reveals regular quadrilateral diffraction spots, suggesting that the SI<sub>4</sub> possesses a uniform single-crystal structure. It also reveals the existence of (002), (200), and (202) facets in the [010] zone axis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c). The energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mappings in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d–f illustrate that the SI<sub>4</sub>@C material, measuring approximately 0.5 μm in size, features a 2D nanostructure. Within the carbon nanocage, there is a uniform distribution of dense C, S, and I components. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g–i) vividly illustrates the crystalline core and the nanosized amorphous texture of the outer carbon shell, boasting a diameter ranging from 3 to 5 nm. For SI<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i), the lattice-resolved HRTEM image shows an interplanar spacing of 3.6 Å for the (200) planes and 2.6 Å for the (002) planes. Among them, the interplanar spacing of 2.6 Å cannot match the lattice pattern of an orthorhombic phase iodine well, which may be due to the phase transition of SI<sub>4</sub> under exposure to a high-energy electron beam or the addition of S that damages the original lattice structure of I<sub>2</sub>. The SI<sub>4</sub>@C compound shows a distinct single-crystal structure with a consistent elemental composition, while the carbon shell exhibits a uniform nanoscale annular morphology, effectively encapsulating the active SI<sub>4</sub> material. A multitude of well-dispersed SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheets can be observed within the fibers, as confirmed by AFM analysis. These nanosheets consist of only several layers, with the height profile of the SI<sub>4</sub>@C sheets estimated to be approximately 10.0 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The structure, composition, and surface chemical states of the nanoflakes were investigated by Raman, X-ray diffraction (XRD), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), and the results reveal no other impurities. The Raman spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a) reveals the chemical structure of SI<sub>4</sub>@C (ranges from 100 to 200 cm<sup>–1</sup>) that displays two types of bands at ∼110.1, 189, and 180 cm<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to I<sub>3</sub><sup>–</sup>, and I<sub>2</sub> stretching modes, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The broadband at 420–435 cm<sup>–1</sup> may be related to the formation of S–S (432 cm<sup>–1</sup>) and I–I (423 cm<sup>–1</sup>) stretching modes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> All XRD reflection peaks can be accurately indexed as belonging to a pure orthorhombic phase of iodine (JCPDS card no. 43–0304) with lattice constants of <i>a</i> = 7.4 Å, <i>b</i> = 4.5 Å, and <i>c</i> = 9.6 Å (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). It is notable that the (200) and (400) peaks exhibit significantly higher intensity magnitudes compared to the remaining peaks, indicating a distinct preferred orientation in the sample. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> XPS curves of the SI<sub>4</sub>@C display the existence of C, I, S, and O without any impurities (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). The high-resolution C 1s spectrum shows one primary and two subpeaks centered at 284.8, 286.2, and 288.7, corresponding to C═C/C–C, C–O, and C═O/S, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The corresponding high-resolution spectrum shows that there are two XPS peaks of I 3d with binding energies at 618.7 eV (I 3d<sub>5/2</sub>) and 630.2 eV (I 3d<sub>3/2</sub>), confirming the presence of elemental iodine in the SI<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). The high-resolution I 3d spectrum of the fresh SI<sub>4</sub>@C cathode is mainly composed of I<sup>0</sup>, as evidenced by the I 3d<sub>5/2</sub> peak located at 618.7 eV. Two extra I 3d<sub>5/2</sub> peaks located at 620.5 and 615.9 eV correspond to the I–O bond and I–C bond, respectively, which may come from the carbon cloth. Meanwhile, the presence of C–I (629.1 and 615.9 eV) may be related to the formation of the I–C chemical bond. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The fitting S 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f) shows two divided bands, where two bands at 165.0 and 163.8 eV are assigned to S–S bonds.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/medium/am4c05252_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Morphological and structural characterization of SI<sub>4</sub>@C. (a) SEM of SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheets. (b) TEM image and (c) SAED of SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheet. (d–f) EDS mapping images indicate the homogeneous elemental distribution of C, I and S, confirming the chemical compositions of the SI<sub>4</sub>@C. (g, h) HRTEM images of SI<sub>4</sub>@C, showing its core–shell structure, and (i) partial enlargement TEM image of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c05252&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/medium/am4c05252_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Structural and compositional characterizations of SI<sub>4</sub>@C. (a) Raman spectrum of the SI<sub>4</sub>@C. (b) XRD patterns of the SI<sub>4</sub>@C. (c) XPS survey spectra of SI<sub>4</sub>@C. (d) C 1s, (e) I 3d, and (f) S 2p spectra of SI<sub>4</sub>@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c05252&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To assess their electrochemical properties, we conducted electrochemical measurements including cyclic voltammetry (CV), galvanostatic, and rate cycling. For CV measurements, a 2025 coin-cell assembly comprising SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth cathode, Na anode, and a carbonate-based electrolyte was employed. Notably, when the scan rate is at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the initial CV profiles of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon show three pairs of redox peaks at 3.6/3.5, 3.2/3.0, and 2.1/2.0 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). Meanwhile, the CV of pure carbon cloth has almost no redox peak in the voltage range of 1.0–3.8 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). As expected, the largest peaks at 2.1/2.0 V correspond to the S ⇆ Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub><i>n</i></sub> ⇆ Na<sub>2</sub>S conversion, while peaks at 3.2/3.0 and 3.6/3.5 V are attributed to the I<sub>2</sub> ⇆ NaI<sub>3</sub> and NaI<sub>3</sub> ⇆ NaI couples, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The overall redox reactions of SI<sub>4</sub>@C thus can be described as SI<sub>4</sub>@C + 6Na<sup>+</sup> + 6e<sup>–</sup> ⇆ Na<sub>6</sub>SI<sub>4</sub>@C. In brief, CV results reveal two couples of two-electron transfers of S<sup>0</sup> ⇆ S<sup>2–</sup> and I<sub>2</sub><sup>0</sup> ⇆ 2I<sup>–</sup> in the redox processes of SI<sub>4</sub>@C, indicating a high specific capacity potential. Meanwhile, similar CV curves measured at an increasing scan rate (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a) without obvious peak shifts indicate high redox reversibility with excellent reaction kinetics. Moreover, CV curves of Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth obtained at scan rates from 1.0 to 5.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> exhibit almost no obvious peak shifts, indicating excellent redox reversibility. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b presents the charge/discharge curves of the Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth cathodes at different current densities. At a current density of 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the cathode exhibits a stable voltage plateau, indicating fast redox kinetics during the charge/discharge process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c shows the rate performance of the Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth. In particular, Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth demonstrates exceptional specific capacities of 332.7, 289.5, 264.1, 235.4, and 215.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at high current densities of 1.0, 3.0, 5.0, 7.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which indicates faster electrochemical reaction kinetics of the Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C system. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d shows the charge and discharge curves with different cycles. After 500 cycles at a current density of 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the cathode exhibits a stable voltage plateau, indicating fast redox kinetics during the charge/discharge process. Furthermore, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e illustrates the cycling performance of the Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth cells at a pressure of 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. After 500 cycles, the capacity was measured to be 217.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth. It is important to note that TEM analysis further confirms the well-preserved 2D nanostructure of cycled SI<sub>4</sub>@C, wherein the SI<sub>4</sub>@C component with high crystallinity remains fully encapsulated by carbon nanocages. In comparison to the SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth cathode, the naked S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil exhibits inferior rate performance, characterized by lower specific capacities and less capacity retention. These findings suggest that the self-healing SI<sub>4</sub>@C composite demonstrates superior electrochemical activity and structural stability when compared to the naked S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). The capacity retention of S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil is much lower at high current rates because of the poor intrinsic electrical conductivity. By contrast, the SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth cathode has less capacity loss from 1.0 to 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> than S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil, exhibiting superb rate capabilities. Thus, the 2D SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheet structure improves the specific surface area, exposes more electrochemical active sites, and shows excellent specific capacity and rate performance, thus achieving greatly improved electrochemical energy storage performance compared with the S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil. In addition, carbon cloth also contributes a small amount of capacity. Na–carbon cloth cell demonstrates specific capacities of 44.7, 21.5, 12.6, 12.5, and 11.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 1.0, 3.0, 5.0, 7.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a</a>) and a reversible capacity of 15.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b</a>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/medium/am4c05252_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) CV curves of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth in the voltage window of 1.0–3.8 V with different scan rates ranging from 1.0 to 5.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Discharge–charge curves at different current densities of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth. (c) Rate performance at different current densities of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth. (d) Discharge–charge curves at different cycles of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth. (e) Cycling performance of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/asset/images/large/am4c05252_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c05252&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SI<sub>4</sub>@C features a low melting point of around 65 °C, which enables repairing of damaged interfaces due to cycling by periodical remelting of the cathode material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> In order to repair the damaged interfaces, the SI<sub>4</sub>@C was irradiated by the high-energy electron beam of in situ TEM. After a long time of irradiation on the damaged area, the heat generated by high-energy electron beams gradually repaired the damaged lattice structure of SI<sub>4</sub>@C in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. Therefore, as demonstrated in the cycling stability shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, reheating is highly effective in healing the cathode interfaces. When cycled at a current density of 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity of the SI<sub>4</sub>@C cathode decays during the initial three cycles (1st:330.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> to 3rd: 306.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). This fade is attributed to the degradation of the cathode interfaces and can be addressed by briefly remelting the cathode at 70 °C before cycling again at 30 °C in which the Na–SI<sub>4</sub>@C cell capacity recovers to ∼330 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the recycled SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth cathode after long-term charge and discharge has little change compared with that of the as-prepared electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). SEM and TEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a–c</a>) further confirm the well-maintained lamellar nanostructure of SI<sub>4</sub>@C after cycling, where the S–I component with high crystallinity is still encapsulated in the carbon shells (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12d</a>). In order to further study the effect of the shuttle effect on the corrosion degree of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth and S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil electrodes during the electrochemical reaction, SEM was used to monitor their surface states after a long period. By comparing the SEM images above with <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c05252/suppl_file/am4c05252_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, we can conclude that the degree of corrosion of the Na anode by the S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil electrode is much greater than that of the SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth. Although corrosion cannot be completely avoided, it is mitigated by the introduction of S–C, I–C, and carbon cloth. These findings confirm that SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheets contribute to improved long-term cycling stability and rate performance due to their excellent structural integrity and low solubility in organic electrolytes.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, 2D SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheet has been developed by a solvothermal reaction, which synergistically consolidates S, I<sub>2</sub>, and C within a nanocomposite. Such a design enhances the structural stability and electrical conductivity of SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth cathodes. Through the simultaneous nucleation of carbon encapsulation shells and crystalline SI<sub>4</sub> cores, nanocomposite cathode materials with exceptional compactness, conductivity, and structural stability have been achieved. The superior Na<sup>+</sup>-ion migration observed in the SI<sub>4</sub>@C cathode is attributed to the synergistic effect of the S–I elemental components in a 2D crystalline state. This consolidation design not only significantly boosts the electrochemical performance of S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub>-based cathodes but also ensures structural diversity and flexibility, particularly with a high mass loading. The utilization of SI<sub>4</sub>@C in batteries holds the promise of enhancing energy density, power density, cycling stability, and safety, thereby representing a crucial advancement in the realm of future batteries. Furthermore, the straightforward and scalable fabrication approach for this cathode material suggests its potential for commercialization in S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub>-based batteries. In the future, 2D nanomaterials based on S<sub><i>x</i></sub>I<sub><i>y</i></sub> will hold immense application potential in diverse fields such as nanotechnology, electronics, optoelectronics, and energy storage due to their unique structures and properties.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c05252?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c05252</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">DSC of sulfur iodide; SEM images of pure carbon cloth; contact angles of the S/I<sub>2</sub>–Al foil, pure-carbon cloth, and SI<sub>4</sub>@C-carbon cloth in the electrolyte solution; AFM topographical image and height profile of SI<sub>4</sub>@C nanosheets;                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Design strategies for nonaqueous multivalent-ion and monovalent-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Matthew; Lu, Jun; Ji, Xiulei; Li, Yanguang; Shao, Yuyan; Chen, Zhongwei; Zhong, Cheng; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Reviews Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">276-294</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NRMADL</span>;

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The inability of current battery technologies to keep up with the performance requirements of industry is pushing forward developments in electrochem.  Specifically, the batteries neg. electrode, the anode, presents many unique chem., phys. and engineering challenges.  Lithium-based battery technologies have dominated the past decade, but concerns about the limited supply of lithium in the Earths crust have led researchers to look towards alternative metal-ion technologies.  Various alkali metals (such as sodium and potassium) and alkali earth metals (such as magnesium and calcium) have attracted significant research interest.  In this Review, we analyze these technologies in a coherent manner, addressing the problems of each type of anode, rather than those of specific types of metal-ion batteries.  Covering direct metal plating and stripping, intercalation-based, alloy-based and conversion-reaction-based anode technologies, this anal. will offer the reader a comprehensive understanding of the behavior of different metal-ion anodes and of what can be learned by transferring knowledge between these different systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700488"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Unique Structural Design and Strategies for Germanium-Based Anode Materials Toward Enhanced Lithium Storage</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1700488</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201700488</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2017%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DD.%2BLi%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DT.%2BZhou%26author%3DW.%2BZhang%26author%3DH.%2BK.%2BLiu%26author%3DZ.%2BGuo%26title%3DUnique%2BStructural%2BDesign%2Band%2BStrategies%2Bfor%2BGermanium-Based%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2BToward%2BEnhanced%2BLithium%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201700488&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c05252&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201700488&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">General Liquid-Driven Coaxial Flow Focusing Preparation of Novel Microcapsules for Rechargeable Magnesium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lin, Xirong; Liu, Jinyun; Zhang, Haikuo; Zhong, Yan; Zhu, Mengfei; Zhou, Ting; Qiao, Xue; Zhang, Huigang; Han, Tianli; Li, Jinjin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Science (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2002298</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASDCCF</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Magnesium batteries have been considered promising candidates for next-generation energy storage systems owing to their high energy d., good safety without dendrite formation, and low cost of magnesium resources.  However, high-performance cathodes with stable capacity, good cond., and fast ions transport are needed, since many conventional cathodes possess a low performance and poor prepn. controllability.  Herein, a liq.-driven coaxial flow focusing (LDCFF) approach for prepg. a novel microcapsule system with controllable size, high loading, and stable magnesium-storage performance is presented.  Taking the MoS2-infilled microcapsule as a case study, the magnesium battery cathode based on the microcapsules displays a capacity of 100 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles.  High capacity retention is achieved at both low and high temps. of -10, -5, and 45°C, and a stable rate-performance is also obtained.  The influences of the liq. flow rates on the size and shell thickness of the microcapsules are investigated; and electron and ion diffusion properties are also studied by first-principle calcns.  The presented LDCFF method is quite general, and the high performance of the microcapsules enables them to find broad applications for making emerging energy-storage materials and secondary battery systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701592"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Guidelines and trends for next-generation rechargeable lithium and lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Feixiang; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Com. lithium-ion (Li-ion) batteries suffer from low energy d. and do not meet the growing demands of the energy storage market.  Therefore, building next-generation rechargeable Li and Li-ion batteries with higher energy densities, better safety characteristics, lower cost and longer cycle life is of outmost importance.  To achieve smaller and lighter next-generation rechargeable Li and Li-ion batteries that can outperform com. Li-ion batteries, several new energy storage chemistries are being extensively studied.  In this review, we summarize the current trends and provide guidelines towards achieving this goal, by addressing batteries using high-voltage cathodes, metal fluoride electrodes, chalcogen electrodes, Li metal anodes, high-capacity anodes as well as useful electrolyte solns.  We discuss the choice of active materials, practically achievable energy densities and challenges faced by the resp. battery systems.  Furthermore, strategies to overcome remaining challenges for achieving energy characteristics are addressed in the hope of providing a useful and balanced assessment of current status and perspectives of rechargeable Li and Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C7CS00139H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, H.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Review of Flexible Lithium-Sulfur and Analogous Alkali Metal-Chalcogen Rechargeable Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review of flexible lithium-sulfur and analogous alkali metal-chalcogen rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Hong-Jie; Huang, Jia-Qi; Zhang, Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
